Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21803&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">To design a high-performance sodium-ion battery anode, binary zinc phosphides (ZnP<sub>2</sub> and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>) were synthesized by a facile solid-state heat treatment process, and their Na storage characteristics were evaluated. The Na reactivity of ZnP<sub>2</sub> was better than that of Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>. Therefore, a C-modified ZnP<sub>2</sub>-based composite (ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C) was fabricated to achieve better electrochemical performance. To investigate the electrochemical reaction mechanism of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during sodiation/desodiation, various ex situ analytical techniques were employed. During sodiation, ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the composite was transformed into NaZn<sub>13</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>P phases, exhibiting a one-step conversion reaction. Conversely, Zn and P in NaZn<sub>13</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>P, respectively, were fully recombined to the original ZnP<sub>2</sub> phase during desodiation. Owing to the one-step conversion/recombination of ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the composite during cycling, the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C showed high electrochemical performance with a highly reversible capacity of 883 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 130 cycles with no capacity deterioration and a fast C-rate capability of 500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 C and 350 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3 C.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D15053%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DKi-Hun%2BNam%252C%2BYoon%2BHwa%252C%2BCheol-Min%2BPark%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D13%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b21803%26title%3DZinc%2BPhosphides%2Bas%2BOutstanding%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%2BAnodes%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D15062%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292246&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Extended X-ray absorption fine structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Extended%20X-ray%20absorption%20fine%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Extended X-ray absorption fine structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Physical%20and%20chemical%20processes" class="article__tags__link">Physical and chemical processes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291333&amp;ref=fulltext" title="X-ray absorption near edge spectroscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_X-ray%20absorption%20near%20edge%20spectroscopy" class="article__tags__link">X-ray absorption near edge spectroscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Zinc+Phosphide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_zinc%20phosphide">zinc phosphide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Phosphorus-based+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_phosphorus-based%20anode">phosphorus-based anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Zinc-based+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_zinc-based%20anode">zinc-based anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Metal+Phosphide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_metal%20phosphide">metal phosphide</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Among rechargeable battery systems, interest in the sodium-ion battery (SIB), which is a promising alternative to the Li-ion battery (LIB), has exploded recently thanks to sodium’s low market cost, natural abundance on the earth’s crust, and chemical similarity to Li. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Despite the similar characteristics of Li and Na, which both belong to the same alkali group in the periodic table, conventional graphite anodes are not suitable for SIBs because Na ions (1.02 Å) have a larger ionic radii than that of Li ions (0.76 Å) and thus, Na ions cannot intercalate into the interlayer channels of graphite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> In addition, elemental Na has a relatively higher standard reduction potential (−2.71 V for Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) than that of elemental Li (−3.04 V for Li<sup>+</sup>/Li). For these reasons, other carbonaceous and Na-alloyable materials with high Na-reversible capacities (RCs) have been suggested as encouraging candidates for new SIB anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 7 to 11">(7−11)</a> Since the finding of our group that black phosphorus (P) with a puckered-layer structure can be utilized as a high capacity LIB anode material, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> P-based materials have been studied widely as high-capacity LIB and SIB anodes by many researchers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 13 to 20">(13−20)</a> Furthermore, P has three main allotropes (red P, white P, and black P) as well as the attractive merits of low price and high theoretical capacity (Li<sub>3</sub>P and Na<sub>3</sub>P: 2595 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). Therefore, with the exception of white P, which has highly reactive and toxic properties, orthorhombic black P and amorphous red P, which are chemically stable, have been widely investigated as anode materials for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 13 to 20">(13−20)</a> However, P-based electrodes experience a large volume change (∼490%) during sodiation/desodiation due to the formation of a Na<sub>3</sub>P phase, resulting in poor electrochemical performance. Recently, many researchers have reported that metal phosphides, such as Si-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Ge-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> Sn-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 24 to 30">(24−30)</a> Fe-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 31 to 33">(31−33)</a> Co-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 34 to 37">(34−37)</a> Cu-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref49" aria-label="reference 38 and 49">(38,49)</a> Ni-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> V-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Mo-based, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> and Zn-based <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> phosphides are able to mitigate the volume changes and to improve electrochemical conductivity. On the other hand, zinc (Zn) is also an interesting element owing to its abundance, low price, and high electrical conductivity (1.69 × 10<sup>7</sup> S m<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Furthermore, Zn can be electrochemically alloyed with Li, resulting in a LiZn phase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> In SIBs, Zn also can be electrochemically alloyed with small amounts of Na. Despite the small theoretical capacity of Zn, several Zn-based materials, such as Zn<sub>4</sub>Sb<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a> ZnSb, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> ZnSnO<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> ZnSnS<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> and ZnO, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> have been reported for SIB anodes. These materials have shown interesting electrochemical performance because the Zn in these materials contributes to the relaxation of the volume change during Na-cycling and the improvement of electrochemical conductivity.</div><div class="NLM_p">The binary Zn–P system has various compounds and polymorphs, such as ZnP<sub>2</sub> (α-tetragonal, β-cubic, monoclinic), Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> (α-tetragonal, β-cubic), and ZnP<sub>4</sub> (monoclinic). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> Thanks to their interesting characteristics and various crystalline structures, they have been studied widely for optoelectronics, semiconductors, photovoltaic devices, and Schottky diodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 55 and 56">(55,56)</a> Furthermore, binary zinc phosphides have been applied as LIB anode materials because of the high theoretical capacities of ZnP<sub>2</sub> (1474 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> (934 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), and ZnP<sub>4</sub> (1841 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57 ref58 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 57 to 59">(57−59)</a> Considering their wide use in LIB applications and high theoretical capacities (ZnP<sub>2</sub>: 1279 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>: 647 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) for SIBs, zinc phosphides also can be applied as high-capacity SIB anode materials. However, zinc phosphides have hitherto not received any attention as new SIB anode materials.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, to utilize the synergetic effects of both P and Zn, we synthesized the binary zinc phosphides, ZnP<sub>2</sub> and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> via a facile solid-state heat treatment process, and their suitability as anode materials for SIBs was tested. Moreover, a C-modified composite (ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C) was also fabricated using an additional ball milling (BM) technique. The electrochemical reaction mechanisms of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during sodiation/desodiation were investigated using various ex situ analytical techniques, such as X-ray diffraction (XRD), high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM), X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES), and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS).</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Zn–P binary compounds (ZnP<sub>2</sub> and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>) were synthesized using a facile solid-state synthetic process (heat treatment) with stoichiometric amounts of bulk Zn (99.99%, ∼150 μm, Aldrich) and red P (99%, ∼75 μm, Aldrich) powders. Details of the solid-state heat treatment synthetic method are as follows: hand-mixed powders were uniaxially pressed into coin-shaped pellets under a pressure of 3 MPa. The pellets were heat treated in a quartz tube furnace at 700 °C for 5 h under a high-purity Ar atmosphere with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. To obtain the C-modified composites (Zn-C and ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C), the BM process was conducted as follows: a hardened-steel vial containing the powders [the as-synthesized ZnP<sub>2</sub> and carbon black (Super P)] and stainless-steel balls was assembled in a high-purity Ar-filled glove box. The assembled vial was taken out from the glove box and then ball milled via a SPEX-8000 mill/mixer for 6 h at a motor speed of approximately 1425 rpm. Although the BM process is very advantageous for producing various C-modified composites, it is difficult to produce in large quantities. On the basis of preliminary electrochemical performance measurements [e.g., initial Coulombic efficiencies (ICEs), RCs, and cycling stability], optimum levels of the composites (Zn-C and ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C) were chosen with a weight ratio of active material to carbon black of 70:30.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure and phase of the active materials (Zn, black P, ZnP<sub>2</sub>, and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>) were identified by XRD (D/max 2500 PC, Rigaku). Their particle size and morphology were analyzed using a particle size analyzer (PSA, Mastersizer-2000) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM, JSM-6500, JEOL, acceleration voltage: 20 kV). In-depth structural and morphological characteristics were analyzed by HR-TEM (JEM-ARM200F, JEOL, acceleration voltage: 200 kV) and energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) spectroscopy affixed to the HR-TEM. The ex situ analytical techniques, such as XRD, HR-TEM, and Zn K-edge XANES and EXAFS, were used to examine the electrochemically driven structural phase changes of the active materials in the electrodes during sodiation/desodiation. The X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) spectra were attained via beamline 7D at the Pohang Accelerator Laboratory (PAL, South Korea). The XAS spectra encompassing both XANES and EXAFS were collected at the Zn K-edge in the transmittance mode and recorded with the storage energy of 3.0 GeV and current of 300 mA in the top-up mode.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All electrodes comprised active materials, Denka black as a conductive agent, and polyvinyl alcohol/polyacrylic acid as a binder in a mass ratio of 8:1:1. The electrode slurry was cast onto substrates (Cu foil) with a doctor blade and vacuum-dried at 120 °C overnight. Then, each electrode was cut to the required size; the average areal loading density of the electrodes was maintained at ∼2.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The assembly of coin-type cells was conducted in an Ar-filled glove box and they consisted of a metallic Na foil as the counter/reference electrode, a separator (glass fiber GF/D, Whatman), and an electrolyte [0.7 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/diethylene carbonate (1:1 v/v) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate]. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data were recorded using a ZIVE-MP2A (ZIVE LAB). Galvanostatic battery cycling tests of the cells were performed using a Maccor-4000 series cycler.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Zn and P can be alloyed into binary Zn–P compounds, such as ZnP<sub>2</sub>, Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>, and ZnP<sub>4</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref57" aria-label="reference 54 and 57">(54,57)</a> However, the ZnP<sub>2</sub> and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> phases only exist as binary Zn–P compounds in the Zn–P binary phase diagram (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> which means that Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> and ZnP<sub>2</sub> are stable phases. Therefore, a facile solid-state heat treatment process was performed to synthesize Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> and ZnP<sub>2</sub>. The XRD pattern of the synthesized Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> coincided exactly with its standard pattern (JCPDS #74-1145, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a). However, the XRD pattern of the synthesized ZnP<sub>2</sub> suggested the coexistence of two polymorphs: the tetragonal (JCPDS #72-1626) and monoclinic (JCPDS #24-1463) phases (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). Thus, an additional BM process was applied to the heat-treated polymorphic ZnP<sub>2</sub>, and interestingly, it was then transformed into tetragonal ZnP<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS #72-1626), which is its high-pressure phase (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). All observed XRD patterns of ZnP<sub>2</sub> corresponded exactly to the tetragonal ZnP<sub>2</sub> phase, which shows that the coexisting polymorphic ZnP<sub>2</sub> phases were transformed successfully through the BM process. The conditions of pressure (∼6 GPa) and temperature (∼200 °C) generated during the BM processing enabled its transformation to the high-pressure phase. Moreover, the results of the Rietveld refinement of the XRD patterns of synthesized Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> and ZnP<sub>2</sub> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b. The refined lattice parameter values of Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> [<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.1173 Å, <i>c</i> = 11.34 Å with reliability index (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub>) of 10.4%] and ZnP<sub>2</sub> (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 5.0681 Å, <i>c</i> = 18.5415 Å with <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> of 12.5%) are matched well with their standard values. The crystal structures of tetragonal Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> (S.G. <i>P</i>4<sub>2</sub>/<i>nmc</i>) and ZnP<sub>2</sub> (S.G. <i>P</i>4<sub>1</sub>2<sub>1</sub>2) are also shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b, respectively. The Zn K-edge EXAFS spectra of Zn metal, ZnP<sub>2</sub>, and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> were obtained by the Fourier-transformation (FT) of <i>k</i><sup>3</sup> χ(<i>k</i>) using the Athena software, which are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a,b</a>. Additionally, the experimental EXAFS fitting was conducted using the Artemis software with included the FEFF tool on the basis of crystallographic information. The main Zn–P peaks determined by EXAFS were ∼1.93 Å (ZnP<sub>2</sub>) and ∼1.85 Å (Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>), respectively. However, the peak position was shifted to ∼0.3–0.4 Å left for the real bond length because the phase shift by rescattering of photoelectrons during FT was not taken into account. These Zn–P peaks were clearly distinguished from the main Zn–Zn peaks of Zn metal (∼2.3 Å). Moreover, the EXAFS fitting results of ZnP<sub>2</sub> and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> are in good agreement with those of proposed model structures, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Synthesis and electrochemical performance of binary Zn–P compounds. (a) Rietveld-refined XRD patterns of Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> and its standard (JCPDS #74-1145). (b) Rietveld-refined XRD patterns of ZnP<sub>2</sub> and its standard (JCPDS #72-1626). (c) Discharge–charge curves of Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> (current density: 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). (d) Discharge–charge curves of ZnP<sub>2</sub> (current density: 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21803&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the Na-ion storage characteristics of synthesized Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> and ZnP<sub>2</sub>, the discharge–charge (sodiation/desodiation) curves of black P, Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>, and ZnP<sub>2</sub> electrodes are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S4b</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d, respectively (voltage range: 0–2.0 V, current density: 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). First, the black P (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>) synthesized by a solid-state BM process for 12 h showed a high sodiation capacity of 2436 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>). However, its desodiation capacity was comparatively poor, which was caused by a massive volume variation due to the formation of a Na<sub>3</sub>P phase and poor electrical conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 13 to 20">(13−20)</a> The Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> showed a quite low 1st sodiation capacity of 377 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c), which was influenced by the low sodiation potential and the high overpotential required to break the interatomic Zn–P bonds in Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> during sodiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61 ref62 ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref62 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 61 to 64">(61−64)</a> On the other hand, ZnP<sub>2</sub> showed a high 1st sodiation capacity of 1260 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Considering the theoretical capacity of ZnP<sub>2</sub> (1279 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, calculated based on NaZn<sub>13</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>P as the final phases at room temperature), it almost fully reacted with Na. However, the desodiation capacity of ZnP<sub>2</sub> was 121 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which is too small for use as a SIB anode material. Additionally, ZnP<sub>2</sub> exhibited 1st sodiation capacity of 817 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), which demonstrates that the reversibility of ZnP<sub>2</sub> is affected to the current density.</div><div class="NLM_p">According to previous studies, the combination of Na-alloy-based and carbonaceous materials has been a promising approach to enhance the Na reactivity and reversibility of Na-alloy-based materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68" aria-label="reference 9, 10 and 65 to 68">(9,10,65−68)</a> The carbonaceous materials in the composite improve the electronic conductivity, thereby improving electrochemical Na reactivity and reversibility, and act as a matrix to counteract volume changes during cycling. Therefore, ZnP<sub>2</sub>, having better Na reactivity than Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>, was selected and used to fabricate a carbon-modified composite (ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C) using the synthesized ZnP<sub>2</sub> and carbon black powders via a further solid-state BM process. The XRD patterns of the fabricated ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C matched only those of the ZnP<sub>2</sub> standard (JCPDS #72-1626) with much reduced crystallinity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). The average crystallite size of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C was approximately 10.7 nm, which was calculated based on the three main planes by the Scherrer equation. The Zn K-edge EXAFS spectrum of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C also matched that of the pristine ZnP<sub>2</sub> with the main peak of ∼1.93 Å (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). To investigate the morphological characteristics of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C, a SEM image with a PSA result and HR-TEM images with EDX maps are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c–f, respectively. The morphology and average size of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C particles were examined via SEM and PSA, which reveal that the average particle size of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C was approximately 5 μm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c–e shows bright-field and HR-TEM images with the corresponding diffraction patterns (DPs) and FT patterns of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C. They demonstrate that the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C consisted of 8–12 nm-sized ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites within the amorphous C matrix, which is in well agreement with the result of the Scherrer equation. Furthermore, scanning TEM and EDX maps of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f verify the homogeneous distribution of ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites within the amorphous C matrix. EIS analyses of the ZnP<sub>2</sub> and ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C electrodes before cycling were performed within the frequency range from 100 kHz to 10 mHz with ac signals of 5 mV to investigate their electrochemical resistance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). The Nyquist impedance plot of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C electrode showed a considerably lower charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at high frequency than that of the ZnP<sub>2</sub> electrode. Furthermore, the calculated Na-ion diffusion coefficients of the ZnP<sub>2</sub> and ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C electrodes were 2.48 × 10<sup>–14</sup> and 3.59 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> S<sup>–1</sup>, respectively.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Structural and morphological characteristics of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C. (a) XRD patterns of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C and its reference (JCPDS #72-1626). (b) Zn K-edge EXAFS spectra of ZnP<sub>2</sub> and ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C. (c) Bright-field TEM image, (d) HR-TEM image with DPs. (e) FT patterns corresponding to the selected regions in (d). (f) Scanning TEM image and its corresponding EDX elemental maps (Zn: green, P: red, C: blue).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21803&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To evaluate the electrochemical Na-storage behavior of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C as a SIB anode material, the discharge–charge curves for the 1st and 2nd cycles of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C anode (current density: 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. The 1st sodiation/desodiation capacity was 1070/704 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with an ICE of 65.8%, which is significantly better than that of the ZnP<sub>2</sub> anode. Considering the 1st irreversible capacity of the BM-C (30 wt %, 124 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a,b</a>), nanocrystalline–ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite had a high Na-reversible reaction, taking into account the irreversible capacity contributed by the interface layer formed between the electrode and electrolyte. The CV plots of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C showed a large peak during sodiation/desodiation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). To evaluate the significantly improved Na reversibility, the sodiation/desodiation mechanism of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C was investigated by ex situ analytical techniques, such as ex situ XRD and XAS (XANES and EXAFS), based on the points indicated in the CV plots (<i>t</i><sub>0</sub>–<i>t</i><sub>2</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). Ex situ XRD peaks of ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the composite (<i>t</i><sub>0</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) were transformed into the Na<sub>3</sub>P phase after full sodiation (<i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) without any trace of a Na–Zn-related phase. Therefore, the variations in the valence state and local structural phase were also verified by ex situ Zn K-edge XANES and EXAFS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d–f). At the fully sodiated state (<i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), the Zn K-edge XANES spectrum of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C showed that the absorption edge, E<sub>0</sub> was shifted to a lower energy. Additionally, it did not match that of pristine Zn (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). Specifically, the derivative absorption plots (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e), which were derived from the XANES spectra, show that the peak of the absorption edge, E<sub>0</sub> (9660.2 eV, ZnP<sub>2</sub>, <i>t</i><sub>0</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e) clearly shifted to the left (9658.8 eV, <i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). Additionally, the main EXAFS peak shifted to ∼2.27 Å (<i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f), which also did not correspond to Zn metal (Zn–Zn peak: ∼2.3 Å). These XANES and EXAFS data demonstrate that a Na–Zn-related phase was formed at the fully sodiated state of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C. Therefore, to verify the Na–Zn-related phase at the fully sodiated state of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C, the sodiation/desodiation mechanism of bulk Zn and synthesized Zn-C nanocomposite was further analyzed via ex situ XRD and XAS (XANES and EXAFS). Bulk Zn with tens of micron-sized particles and well-developed crystallinity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9a</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a) showed a 1st sodiation/desodiation capacity of 58/14 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), which is a negligible value considering the capacity contributed by the conducting agent, carbon black. Additionally, the ex situ XRD peaks at the fully sodiated and desodiated states of bulk Zn did not vary (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). These results demonstrate that bulk Zn did not react electrochemically with Na. To confirm the Na reactivity of nanocrystalline Zn, a C-modified composite (Zn-C) with the same ratios as the as-synthesized ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C was fabricated using bulk Zn and amorphous C by an additional BM process. The Zn-C composite was composed of secondary particles with an average particle size of approximately 11.2 μm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b</a>), and each secondary particle was composed of an agglomeration of very small primary Zn nanocrystallites approximately 16 nm in size. The size of primary Zn nanocrystallites was calculated by means of the Scherrer equation from the main XRD peak of Zn-C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). Interestingly, Zn-C showed a 1st Na RC of 82 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Considering the theoretical capacity and composition of Zn (NaZn<sub>13</sub>: 32 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 70 wt %) and the 1st RC contributed by the C content (62 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 30 wt %, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>) in the composite, nanocrystalline Zn in the Zn-C composite exhibited full Na reactivity. To confirm the Na-reacted phase of nanocrystalline Zn in the Zn-C composite, ex situ XRD, XANES, and EXAFS analyses were also conducted on the fully sodiated and desodiated states. At the fully sodiated state of 0 V, XRD peaks corresponding to the cubic NaZn<sub>13</sub> phase (JCPDS #03-1008) were definitely observed without any residue of Zn (<i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). Additionally, the absorption edge, E<sub>0</sub> of the Zn K-edge in the XANES spectrum of Zn-C shifted to lower energy (<i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e), and the main derivative absorption peak (Zn: 9659.7 eV) also shifted definitely to the left (NaZn<sub>13</sub>: 9658.8 eV, <i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e). Additionally, the Zn–Zn main peak (∼2.3 Å, <i>t</i><sub>0</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f) was slightly shifted to reflect the Na–Zn main peak (∼2.27 Å) of NaZn<sub>13</sub> at the fully sodiated state (<i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f). The EXAFS fitting parameters of NaZn<sub>13</sub> are also in good agreement with that of the proposed model structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11 and Table S2</a>). Subsequently, at the fully desodiated state of 2.0 V, the NaZn<sub>13</sub> phase reverted to the pristine Zn phase with XRD peaks of crystalline Zn (<i>t</i><sub>2</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d), the main absorption edge, E<sub>0</sub> (Zn: 9659.7 eV, <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e), and main Zn–Zn EXAFS peak of Zn (∼2.3 Å, <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f). The ex situ XRD, XANES, and EXAFS results demonstrated that nanocrystalline Zn in the Zn-C composite exhibits the following sodiation/desodiation mechanism, which is shown crystallographically in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>g.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical sodiation/desodiation mechanism of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C. (a) Discharge–charge curves of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during the 1st and 2nd cycles. (b) CV plots of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C. (c) Ex situ XRD results for ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during the 1st sodiation/desodiation. (d) Zn K-edge XANES spectra and (e) derivative absorption plots of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during the 1st sodiation/desodiation. (f) Zn K-edge EXAFS spectra of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during the 1st sodiation/desodiation. (g) Crystallographic phase-change reaction mechanism of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during sodiation/desodiation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21803&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical reaction mechanism of Zn during sodiation/desodiation. (a) XRD patterns of bulk Zn and its reference (JCPDS #04-0831). (b) XRD patterns of Zn-C and its reference (JCPDS #04-0831). (c) Discharge–charge curves of bulk Zn and Zn-C electrodes (current density: 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). (d) Ex situ XRD patterns of Zn-C during the 1st sodiation/desodiation (<i>t</i><sub>0</sub>: pristine electrode, <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>: Na-in 0 V, <i>t</i><sub>2</sub>: Na-out 2 V). (e) Zn K-edge XANES spectra with its derivative absorption plots of Zn-C during the 1st sodiation/desodiation. (f) Zn K-edge EXAFS spectra of Zn-C during the 1st sodiation/desodiation. (g) Crystallographic phase-change reaction mechanism of Zn-C during sodiation/desodiation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21803&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">During the sodiation process<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">During the desodiation process<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">At the fully sodiated state (<i>t</i><sub>1</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C, the main derivative absorption edge, E<sub>0</sub> (9658.8 eV) and main EXAFS peak (∼2.27 Å) exactly matched with those of the NaZn<sub>13</sub> phase, which was revealed by the XANES and main EXAFS results for nanocrystalline Zn in the Zn-C composite at the fully sodiated state. Therefore, during sodiation, tetragonal ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite was directly converted into the cubic NaZn<sub>13</sub> and hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>P phases. Reversely, at the fully desodiated state (<i>t</i><sub>2</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), ex situ XRD peaks were not detected, suggesting a transformation to an amorphous state (<i>t</i><sub>2</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). However, XANES spectra with corresponding derivative absorption plots and EXAFS spectra matched definitely those of ZnP<sub>2</sub> (<i>t</i><sub>2</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d–f), which confirms the recombination reaction of ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite. Therefore, the sodiation/desodiation mechanism of ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite can be explained as follows:</div><div class="NLM_p">During sodiation process<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">During the desodiation process<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">The interesting one-step sodiation/desodiation mechanism of ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite can be described crystallographically (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>g). During sodiation, the tetragonal ZnP<sub>2</sub> is converted to cubic NaZn<sub>13</sub> and hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>P phases. After that, during desodiation, the cubic NaZn<sub>13</sub> and hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>P phases are recombined into the original tetragonal ZnP<sub>2</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To verify the changes in the ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites within the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C electrode during Na cycling, ex situ HR-TEM images with corresponding DPs and FT patterns, taken before and after Na cycling, were acquired. The 8–12 nm-sized ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a) before Na cycling were reduced in size to 3–5 nm after 10 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b), and then they maintained this reduced size (3–5 nm) after 50 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). The interesting crystallite size changes of ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during ongoing Na cycling are also represented schematically in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Morphological changes of the ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C electrodes. Ex situ HR-TEM images with corresponding DPs and FT patterns of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C (a) pristine electrode, (b) after 10 cycles, and (c) after 50 cycles. (d) Schematic representation of crystallite size variations of ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite during ongoing Na cycling.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21803&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C as a SIB anode is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>. The 1st sodiation/desodiation capacity of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C was 1070/704 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with an ICE of 65.8% (voltage range: 0–2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, current density: 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). The Na-cycling performances of ZnP<sub>2</sub>, the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite, and commercial hard-C are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b (cycling rate: 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). Although the ZnP<sub>2</sub> exhibited poor electrochemical performance, the Na-cycling performance of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C exhibited a high RC of 883 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 130 cycles. Notably, its RC was about 3.5 times higher than that of commercial hard-C. The rate-capability behavior of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c,d (1 C rate: 900 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite exhibited high RCs at fast C-rates (1 C: 500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 3 C: 350 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), which was much faster than that of the commercial hard-C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d). Furthermore, ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C showed a stable cycling performance over 200 cycles at a high 1 C-rate (900 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e). Notably, to our knowledge, the excellent cycling performance and rate-capability are one of the best performances among the reported metal phosphide-based anodes for SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 21 to 44">(21−44)</a> The outstanding Na-storage characteristics of the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C were achieved by the preparation of 8–12 nm ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite through the BM process and the additional preparation of the 3–5 nm-sized ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C by repeated conversion/recombination reactions during ongoing Na cycling. These size-reduced ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C accommodated the strain generated during Na cycling and provided short Na-ion diffusion paths, which contributed to the outstanding Na-storage characteristics.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/medium/am9b21803_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performance of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C as SIB anodes. (a) Discharge–charge curves of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C during the 1st to 100th cycles. (b) Cycling performance of ZnP<sub>2</sub>, ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C, and commercial hard-C (cycling rate: 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). (c) Discharge–charge curves of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C at various C-rates. (d) Rate capabilities of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C (1 C rate: 900 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and commercial hard-C (1 C rate: 250 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). (e) Cycling performances of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C and commercial hard-C at high C-rate.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/asset/images/large/am9b21803_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21803&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Binary zinc phosphides, ZnP<sub>2</sub> and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>, were synthesized using red P and Zn powders by a facile solid-state synthetic method, and their electrochemical Na-storage characteristics were tested. ZnP<sub>2</sub>, having better Na reactivity than Zn<sub>2</sub>P<sub>3</sub>, was selected to fabricate a carbon-modified composite (ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C). The electrochemical sodiation/desodiation mechanism of ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C was investigated by various ex situ analytical techniques, such as ex situ XRD, XANES, and EXAFS. The ZnP<sub>2</sub> in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite showed an interesting one-step conversion/recombination mechanism during sodiation/desodiation. Because of this interesting reaction mechanism, the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite exhibited outstanding Na-storage characteristics. The ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite showed a high RC (883 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 130 cycles) with no capacity deterioration and a fast C-rate capability (500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 C and 350 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3 C). Moreover, the outstanding Na-storage characteristics of ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C were accompanied by the preparation of 8–12 nm-sized ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites in the ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite through the BM process and the further preparation of smaller 3–5 nm-sized ZnP<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallites through repeated conversion/recombination reactions during ongoing Na cycling, which provided short Na-ion diffusion paths and facile accommodation of the strain generated during cycling. We expect that this ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C composite with interesting Na-storage characteristics is a promising option as a high-performance SIB anode material.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b21803?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b21803</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Additional Zn–P binary phase diagram; XRD patterns of synthesized ZnP<sub>2</sub>; Zn K-edge EXAFS spectra of Zn, ZnP<sub>2</sub>, and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>; XRD pattern and discharge–charge curves of black P; electrochemical behavior of ZnP<sub>2</sub> electrode; SEM image with a PSA result for ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C; EIS analysis results for ZnP<sub>2</sub> and ZnP<sub>2</sub>-C; electrochemical performance of BM-C; SEM images and PSA results for bulk Zn and Zn-C; ex situ XRD results for bulk Zn; Zn K-edge EXAFS spectra of Zn-C at the fully sodiated state; Zn K-edge EXAFS fitting parameters for ZnP<sub>2</sub> and Zn<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>; Zn-C at the fully sodiated state; and comparison of electrochemical performance of metal phosphide-based materials for SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21803/suppl_file/am9b21803_si_001.pdf">am9b21803_si_001.pdf (1.69 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b21803" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljW65GUW-aujg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Re-search Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Murata, Wataru; Ishikawa, Toru; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Nakayama, Tetsuri; Ogata, Atsushi; Gotoh, Kazuma; Fujiwara, Kazuya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.  The 3-V secondary Na-ion battery possessing environmental and cost friendliness, Na+-shuttlecock hard-carbon/NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell, demonstrates steady cycling performance as next generation secondary batteries and an alternative to Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrp5J70QNujrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhmbwVH3z_Mew"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhtVWjurfM&amp;md5=be5cae72214fa773ada0e3fcca7b49d1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1149/2.0091514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Irisarri, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ponrouch, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palacin, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of hard carbon materials as neg. electrodes for sodium ion batteries is presented, covering not only the electrochem. performance but also the synthetic methods and microstructures.  The relation between the reversible and irreversible capacities achieved and microstructural features is described and illustrated with specific expts. while discussing also the effect of the electrolyte.  A summary of the current knowledge is given while emphasizing the possibility of further performance improvements by thoroughly mastering structure-property relationships and also discussing the main current bottleneck to maximize energy d. in real applications: the first cycle irreversible capacity.  Finally, a short conclusion and perspectives session is provided highlighting necessary developments in the field to turn the present optimistic research prospects into tangible practical products.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqCeDjgmTzOTLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhmbwVH3z_Mew"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslelu73L&amp;md5=57b9d632618cb4472a8dd9169c71d763</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span>. <i>Acc.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg4dRAdYWJidg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1149/1.3607983"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chevrier, V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been proposed as candidates for replacing Li-ion batteries.  The authors examine the viability of Na-ion anode materials based on Na alloys or hard carbons in terms of volumetric energy d.  Due to the bigger size of the Na atom compared to the Li atom, Na alloys would lead to anode materials with about half the volumetric energy d. of their Li analogs.  Volumetric energy densities obtainable with sodiated hard carbons would also be significantly less than those obtainable with lithiated graphite.  These findings highlight the need of novel ideas for Na-ion anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfbyHmIC7uUrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg4dRAdYWJidg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXpt1Ont7c%253D&amp;md5=f132981922c6ce146def4cd54c721e2b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1038/srep35980"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, J.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, C.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Li Topotactic Reaction in Layered SnP<sub>3</sub> for Superior Li-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Sci.
